Pain in Indian Property

It's time for India's property developers to get real. A slew of negative indicators has been stacking up against the sector for at least a year, yet developers are still clinging to hopes of high property prices.

The cost of new residential properties in some areas continues to rise even as the market sours. In New Delhi and Mumbai, for example, prices were up 13% from a year earlier in September, while sales were down by 30% and 24%, respectively, data from real-estate research firm Liases Foras show.

The malaise is partly the result of efforts to prevent a housing-sector collapse. In late 2008, as the property market tottered, the Reserve Bank of India leaned on banks to restructure loans to developers so as to delay repayment, while the government pressured lenders to offer low mortgage rates. Those effects are wearing off -- since May, interest rates have been rising along with inflation, putting borrowers under pressure. Another RBI bailout seems unlikely.

So far, developers' rising debt has covered up for falling sales, but the borrowing is also leaving them increasingly vulnerable.

Though developers may be in a state of denial, investors in their shares are not: The Bombay Stock Exchange's Realty index is down nearly 50% in the past year, compared with a 14% drop in the Sensex. Foreign investors who had proved keen on the long-term India property story in the past are also pulling back. The portion of India's foreign direct investment that is going into real estate has fallen to just over 2% so far this fiscal year, down from an aggregate 7% since 2000.

Eventually, the strain of declining sales volumes will become too much for developers' balance sheets. Unless home prices are allowed to drop, inventory will get very stale. The problems are clear to investors; India's property developers need to wise up too.

-- Harsh Joshi

Europe's Struggle

To Keep Growing

Europe is at risk of a potentially devastating negative feedback loop. The economy is "practically in free fall," says Yves Mersch, a member of the European Central Bank's governing council. ECB President Mario Draghi has warned publicly of a recession. The latest economic data bear out their gloomy assessments. Failure to tackle the sovereign-debt crisis has fueled the downturn, but a new recession makes the crisis even harder to solve.

While investor attention has been focused on Athens and Cannes, the economic headlines have been disastrous. The euro-zone economy could shrink 0.4% in the fourth quarter, judging by the latest Markit euro-zone composite purchasing managers' index, reckons Barclays Capital. This closely watched index fell to 46.5 in October from 49.1 in September. Anything below 50 indicates an economic contraction. France, Spain and Italy are all contracting; Germany is just holding its head above water. But that may not last. New factory orders in Germany fell 4.3% on the month in September, with orders from the euro zone plummeting 12%.

This will make fiscal consolidation much trickier. If the euro-zone economy tips into recession, Italy, Spain, Portugal, Ireland and Belgium are likely to miss deficit targets by an average of two percentage points of gross domestic product over 2012-2014, J.P. Morgan Chase estimates. That could mean the ratio of debt to GDP is around 15 percentage points higher than expected by 2014. Greece's numbers would look even worse. And if these countries respond to recession with yet more austerity, the downturn is likely to be even deeper, pushing debt-to-GDP higher. That, in turn, increases the likelihood they require further help from Europe's bailout facilities, transmitting strain to stronger states.

Indeed, austerity is starting to bite in northern Europe and will intensify next year. The composite PMI reading for France fell to a 30-month low of 45.6 in October, suggesting a 0.2% contraction is in the cards in the fourth quarter, Barclays says. A renewed recession in France could threaten its triple-A rating, Standard & Poor's recently warned. That poses a risk to the triple-A rating of the European Financial Stability Facility, the euro-zone bailout fund. A worsening sovereign-debt crisis will further batter the continent's banks, cutting credit provision. European banks tightened lending conditions in the third quarter and expect to tighten further, an ECB survey shows.

Europe faces three growth shocks: fiscal consolidation, weaker global demand and the debt crisis. Resolving the debt crisis remains crucial to reviving growth. That leaves little option but to push ahead with fiscal tightening and structural reform, making economies more competitive so that they can grow faster. But those policies will take time to yield results -- and time is no longer on the euro zone's side.

-- Richard Barley

Kirin Isn't Watered Down

By Brazil Beer Transaction

Ah, the thirst-quenching properties of an ice-cold cerveja, swallowed whole. Japanese beer and beverage maker Kirin Holdings has gotten its hands on the 49.54% of shares in Brazil's Schincariol it didn't already own.

The deal brings closure to a three-month battle with the company's minority shareholders, who refused to sell after Kirin bought half of Brazil's No. 2 beer company by sales volume earlier in the year.

But perhaps more satisfying for Kirin's shareholders was a statement from managing director Hirotake Kobayashi that the company won't pay for the deal by issuing more stock.

The deal, paid with around $1.3 billion in cash and loans, increases Kirin's debt-to-equity ratio to 1.10, above its midterm plan to keep it at 1.0 or below. Some analysts had expressed concern that Kirin might issue equity to keep its debt down.

Given that Kirin has averaged almost two acquisitions a year over the past decade, it is positive news for shareholders that Kirin has no intention of using equity financing for further deals right now. In fact, Mr. Kobayashi said that deals financed with debt are unlikely too.

There are other issues for Kirin, whose stock has fallen 16% since it took a position in Schincariol in August. The company on Friday halved its profit forecast for the year.

Then there is the question of the price it has paid for entry to Brazil's growing beer market. The total cost for 100% of Schincariol prices the enterprise value of the company at a multiple of 13 times earnings before interest, taxes, depreciation and amortization for the year through March 2011, according to Citigroup Research. That looks a little rich compared to a multiple of 11 paid by Heineken last year for FEMSA Cervesa.

Management also has yet to prove it can integrate the businesses it has been buying. Kirin has spent nearly $8 billion to expand food and beverage company Lion in Australia without yet generating the margins it has targeted.

Integrating Schincariol will be no easy task. Selling more beer than the previous owners did at better margins in a competitive Brazilian market could prove a sterner test. For now, though, shareholders should be cheered by a deal that doesn't leave them watered down.

-- James Simms

Business
Freddie Again Draws U.S. Assistance
By Nick Timiraos
671 words
4 November 2011
The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
English
Copyright 2011 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.

Freddie Mac tapped the U.S. government for another $6 billion in aid after the mortgage-finance giant's loss widened to $4.4 billion in the third quarter.

The cash drain is the latest sign of how the shaky U.S. housing market continues to bleed red ink for the firm that the government rescued more than three years ago and increased the tab to taxpayers for Freddie alone to $56 billion.

The company needs the new funds to cover the loss, as well as the $1.6 billion that it borrowed in order to pay required quarterly dividends back to the government.

The loss, Freddie's worst quarterly showing in more than one year, compares to a loss of $2.5 billion a year ago. In a statement, Freddie Mac Chief Executive Charles E. Haldeman Jr. attributed the poor performance to "the weak housing market as well as challenging financial market conditions."

Freddie and its cousin, Fannie Mae, were taken over by the government at the height of the credit crisis in 2008. Fannie and Freddie have been pledged unlimited support from the U.S. Treasury through the end of next year, and the government has agreed to inject up to around $300 billion after that to keep the firms afloat and stabilize mortgage markets.

With the most recent draw on Treasury funds, Freddie Mac has now borrowed $71 billion from the government, and it has paid nearly $15 billion back in the form of the 10% annual dividend payments that are required on its government aid. Fannie Mae, which has yet to report its third-quarter results, has taken nearly $104 billion in aid and paid back around $15 billion. The net cost to the taxpayers for bailing out both firms stands at $145 billion.

Freddie's quarterly loss stemmed in part from mark-to-market losses on derivative investments that are used to reduce Freddie Mac's exposure to interest-rate risk. The company recorded a $4.8 billion loss on those investments as interest rates dropped during the July-to-September period.

Standard accounting rules force Freddie to write down the values of the derivative investments, but they aren't able to account for the values of the assets or liabilities that are being hedged. As a result, the investments may not actually lose value over time, but they often result in earnings volatility, showing bigger mark-to-market losses when rates fall and corresponding gains when rates rise.

Potentially more troubling was the $3.6 billion loss that resulted from Freddie's decision to boost its loan-loss reserves for the second straight quarter. That reversed several quarters of declines.

"Are the losses going to accelerate, the deeper they go into their pile of problem loans?" said Jim Vogel, an analyst at FTN Financial.

Losses at Freddie Mac had eased over the last year, with the company returning more money to the government than it borrowed in each of the previous four quarters. The firm's losses continue to stem from loans made as the housing boom turned to bust, and loans being made today are expected to be profitable.

The red ink underscores the continued costs from a housing market that is struggling to find a bottom. While mortgage delinquencies have stopped growing, Freddie and Fannie are now selling tens of thousands of properties that they are taking back through foreclosure. As home prices fall, they will take bigger losses on those sales. Freddie sold around 25,000 homes during the third quarter and acquired another 24,000 through foreclosure.

The company said losses may also be rising because Freddie hasn't been able to recover as much money from mortgage insurance firms that are under financial stress of their own. Last month, for example, state insurance regulators took over PMI Group Inc., one of the nation's largest mortgage insurance companies. Insurance companies reimburse Freddie for a share of losses when loans default.

Chinese Property Collapse 101

What does a meltdown in China's economy look like?

It starts in the property sector, the main driver of domestic growth. Government controls on speculators have already started to bring residential-property prices down. National average prices for residential property fell 0.23% in October from September, the second month in a row of falling prices, according to data released Tuesday by the China Real Estate Index System.

If buyers who are used to prices moving only upward adopt a wait-and-see attitude, then sales volumes will fall. Sales so far this year have been robust, up 12% from a year earlier in the first nine months. But it hasn't always been that way. In 2008, sales fell 15%. A repeat would leave developers short of cash to cover their costs.

The most stressed developers would be forced into a fire sale of inventory, sharply lowering prices to attract buyers. In what could be a sign of things to come, prices at two new developments in Shanghai were cut by more than 20% in October. In a competitive market, once one developer starts to lower prices, everyone will be forced to follow. The result would be a write-down of asset values and lower profit expectations across the sector.

Developers in crisis mode would slow investment. Real-estate investment growth so far in 2011 is tearing along at 35% year to year. But that level of expenditure isn't guaranteed. In the first two months of 2009, year-to-year growth dipped to 4.7%. If sales dry up and credit remains in short supply, a similar decline in 2012 isn't outside the realm of possibility.

A fall of that magnitude in real-estate investment could knock more than two percentage points off China's gross-domestic-product growth, expected to come in around 9% in 2011. The spillover from the slowdown in demand for construction materials, unemployment and the hit to household wealth means the impact could be much greater.

The financial system wouldn't escape the consequences. Around 20% of banks' loan book is directly tied to the real-estate sector. Another 16% has been lent to local governments, which receive 40% of their revenue from land sales. Falling prices for property would knock land prices lower and increase defaults from both sources. With growth slowing and asset quality crumbling, the Chinese government would have a full-fledged crisis on its hands.

That is the worst-case scenario. The central case is that China will muddle through with strong fundamental demand underpinning property prices, and deep government pockets backstopping the banks. But Beijing isn't omniscient. China's property prices are edging down, and the response is difficult to predict. The possibility of a sharp fall in prices spiraling out of the government's control is small -- but it exists. If that happens, it is time to revisit the chances of a hard landing.

-- Tom Orlik

Buyers Must

Beware When

India Sells

India's government is desperate to raise funds by selling bits of state-owned companies. But while New Delhi is a keen seller, potential investors should keep up their guard.

To bolster its finances, India has targeted a handful of divestments it hopes will generate $8 billion this fiscal year. With only five months to go before the March year end, however, New Delhi has raised just 2.9% of its goal. In the meantime, sources of income needed for social-welfare plans and infrastructure projects are drying up. New Delhi's stake sales will take on more urgency, but investors need to scrutinize what is on offer.

Take ONGC, India's largest oil and gas exploration company. The government owns about 74% and planned to unload 5% to the public in September, before turmoil in the markets put the sale on ice.

But anyone buying will have trouble trying to parse the policy risks. ONGC has to pay oil-marketing companies subsidies that are determined by New Delhi; in the April-June quarter, ONGC's discounts to retailers were more than double its net profit, and these subsidies can rise or fall at the government's policy whim.

Over the next four years, India has plans to sell stakes in about 60 state-owned companies. Five, including ONGC, have already been approved for the government's divestment plans. For investors, most carry a degree of policy risk.

-- Harsh Joshi

Greek Move

Deepens

Euro Zone's

Debt Crisis

George Papandreou's decision to call a referendum on Greece's latest debt deal is an astonishing roll of the dice. The Greek prime minister is asking the country to decide whether it wishes to continue with a debt deal that will require Greece to effectively hand over its economic sovereignty to Brussels and endure at least another nine years of brutal austerity at the end of which its debt-to-GDP ratio will still be 120%, higher than Italy today. The alternative is to vote down the debt deal, a path that would likely lead to default, exit from the euro and European Union and economic collapse.

The result has been to throw the euro zone back into turmoil: A referendum is unlikely before January, and current opinion polls suggest 60% of Greeks oppose the deal. Much depends on what question is actually asked and how opposition parties respond. But the decision seems to have caught Brussels by surprise and will no doubt have enraged other euro-zone leaders, coming just days after they cobbled together the latest summit deal, billed as a comprehensive response to the euro crisis.

But in reality, the debt deal was patently inadequate, not only leaving Greece's debt burden at a level that few believe is sustainable but also capping the possibility of future debt relief from the private sector as a result of the 30 billion euro ($41.6 billion) collateral deal agreed on with the banks. Meanwhile, Greece's official lenders, including the European Central Bank, refuse to take losses on their own exposures. Germany and the other northern European countries continue to resist any fiscal transfer -- and it is even argued that to provide one would be illegal under the EU's "no bailout" rules.

Perhaps Mr. Papandreou can convince Greek voters that their destiny remains within the euro. But his decision is a reminder that the future of the euro won't just be determined by leaders at grand summits. Greece has always had the option to repudiate its debts if it considers that in its national self-interest. That in turn will fuel the fear that the euro zone is losing control of this crisis. Certainly, it is hard to see why other countries, such as China, should put their own taxpayers' money at risk to save the euro when there is so much uncertainty over the euro zone's ability to save itself. The warm glow from last week's summit lasted about 48 hours. Mr. Papandreou has ensured the gloom will last for months.

-- Simon Nixon

World News: Economy: U.S. Factory Activity Eases In Step With Global Trend
By Kathleen Madigan
477 words
2 November 2011
The Wall Street Journal Asia
AWSJ
9
English
(c) 2011 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. To see the edition in which this article appeared, click here http://awsj.com.hk/factiva-ns[http://awsj.com.hk/factiva-ns]

The U.S. manufacturing sector barely expanded in October, according to data released Tuesday by the Institute for Supply Management, in a report that follows other disappointing factory figures around the world.

Separately, spending on construction in the U.S. inched ahead during September, though overall the sector continues to struggle with weak demand for new projects.

The ISM's manufacturing purchasing managers' index fell to 50.8 last month from 51.6 in September. A reading above 50 indicates expanding activity.

Economists surveyed by Dow Jones Newswires had expected the October PMI to increase to 52.0.

Purchasing-managers reports around the globe largely show a weak global manufacturing sector as businesses grapple with slowing demand. In particular, the October PMI reported by China's government fell sharply to its lowest level since February 2009.

The U.S. ISM said, "Comments from respondents are mixed, indicating positive relief from raw materials pricing and continuing strength in a few industries, but there is also more concern and caution about growth in this uncertain economy."

Within the U.S. report, the subindexes were generally mixed.

The ISM's new-orders index increased to 52.4 from 49.6, while the production index slowed to 50.1 from 51.2.

The factory-employment index was little changed at 53.5 from 53.8 in September.

The inventory index weakened sharply to 46.7, from 52.0 in September. The exports index fell to 50.0 from 53.5.

Price pressures eased sharply. The prices index plunged to 41.0 from 56.0. The October reading was the first to be below 50 mark since May 2009.

Meanwhile, construction spending in September grew by 0.2% to a seasonally adjusted annual rate of $787.21 billion, the Commerce Department said Tuesday.

Construction spending rose 1.6% in August from July, according to revised figures released Tuesday.

The small September increase shows that construction spending remains held in check by an uncertain economy. New-home sales are near record lows, and municipal governments have been reluctant to spend as they grapple with budget shortfalls.

Year over year, overall construction spending was 1.3% lower than in September 2010. Spending on U.S. residential projects rose 0.7% in September to $236.32 billion compared with the prior month.

New housing starts in September reached the highest level in 17 months, a separate report released last month said. The jump, however, was mainly due to a surge in apartment and condominium construction rather than single-family homes, which grew only slightly. Single-family home construction is the primary driver of residential construction spending.

Real Estate
Miami Condos on Upswing
By Robbie Whelan
793 words
2 November 2011
The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
English
Copyright 2011 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.

The sale of a huge block of vacant apartments in a foreclosed Miami development has marked an important milestone in the city's housing recovery: The condo glut is coming to an end.

Ever since the recession hit, Miami has been awash in thousands of distressed condos left over from the boom years. Slowly, the glut has been absorbed as investors purchased blocks of these units at huge discounts and then rented them out or resold them one by one.

Now a group led by real-estate investor Ronald Krongold has closed on what many believe is the last of these distressed bulk deals. The group purchased 539 units in three buildings that were part of a sprawling $2.3 billion mixed-use project called Midtown Miami, near the city's Design District .

The seller was HSBC Bank USA, which had taken the units back in a friendly foreclosure in July from a group including New York developer Joseph Cayre, who originally built the condo buildings.

The price Mr. Krongold's group paid reflects the improving health of the condo market. At about $110 million, it came to $183 a square foot and 75 cents for each dollar of debt. Price tags on earlier bulk deals were as low as 30 cents to 50 cents on the dollar.

Mr. Krongold declined to specify the price. But he acknowledged the deals aren't as sweet as they used to be. "We did not get a bargain basement price," he said.

Mr. Krongold, who secured financing for the deal from Ladder Capital, a New York commercial real-estate finance firm, plans to rent the units for several years and then sell them.

The rebound in the Miami condo marketwhile housing remains anemic in most other parts of the countryreflects the strong foreign demand for units in Florida's most cosmopolitan city. Average condo prices, which fell to as low as $200 a square foot during the depths of the recession, have risen to about $325 a square foot, according to Scott Wadler, a real estate analyst with HFF L.P.

"The Midtown acquisition represents one of the last remaining 100-plus unit bulk condominium opportunities in all of South Florida," Mr. Wadler says.

Average condominium prices are still well below the peak levels they hit during the go-go years: about $400 to $500 a square foot.

Those prices fueled a development boom that added some 22,000 new apartment units to the city, says HFF. The economic downtown caused thousands of buyers to back out of purchase contracts and left developers saddled with massive debts and entire buildings sitting empty.

At first, banks and other lenders that foreclosed on these developments sold units in bulk. But the overhang steadily shrank.

Of the 22,000 new units added, fewer than 3,500 remain unsold, according to Mr. Wadler. "People are re-launching projects and are selling them retail not wholesale," says Arnaud Karsenti, a principal at 13th Floor Investments who buys real-estate debt.

In Miami's upscale Brickell district, the Icon, a three-tower condo project developed by Jorge Perez's Related Group, announced in October that it had fewer than 20 units of its 1,800 condos left, and expected to sell out in November. Buyers have come from 40 different countries, including Argentina, Colombia, Qatar and Gabon, and prices are between $400 and $500 a square foot.

To be sure, a slowdown in the global economy could put a crimp on demand and slow the Miami condo market.

The pickup in demand has come too late to help some owners, though. For example, Mr. Perez actually had to hand over keys to two of the three Icon towers to his lender in 2010, after running into trouble on $500 million in debt. Related Group stayed on as manager of the project.

But for now, strong demand is bringing back to market once-distressed projects. In early October, an investment partnership including ST Residential, a unit of a team led by Starwood Capital Group, and commercial lender iStar Residential re-launched sales at Paramount Bay, a 47-story waterfront tower.

Starwood and its partners bought the building as part of an FDIC portfolio sale of the assets of failed Corus Bank, which sold for 60 cents on the dollar in 2009.

Now, Paramount Bay's 340 units, which feature interiors designed by rock musician Lenny Kravitz, are selling for $400 a square foot. Miami's priciest buildings were trading for over $600 a square foot at the height of the boom.

"Retail prices are almost back to the good old days," said Edgardo Defortuna, president of Fortune International, a Miami-based brokerage.

Real Estate
'Zombie' Properties Come Back to Life
By Eliot Brown
893 words
2 November 2011
The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
C1
English
Copyright 2011 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.

Four months ago, Ky Nguyen was paying about $6,000 a month in rent for his small, 1,600 square-foot dental office, part of a mostly vacant strip mall in Manteca, Calif.

In July, he bought the entire 25,000 square-foot mall for $1.9 million, one-fifth of what the prior owner paid, purchasing it from a loan servicer that had foreclosed on the property. Now, with an affordable mortgage and some rental revenue, his debt service comes to about $2,000 a month less than he used to pay in rent for his office.

"We jumped on the opportunity," said Mr. Nguyen, a 36-year-old Vietnamese immigrant. He plans to fill much of the vacant space with a restaurant run by his in-laws and a chiropractor's office run by his wife.

Three years after the economy's turn sent commercial property prices tumbling, opportunities for investors are on the rise as lenders and servicers are disposing of a growing volume of distressed loans and foreclosed properties.

The increased flow brings in new owners to "zombie" buildings that have for years been struggling with high vacancy and a lack of investment by owners overwhelmed by debt.

At the same time, though, the new owners pose a threat to existing landlords who are still struggling to get by on boom-year debt levels. Today's investors are able to charge lower rents and still make a profit.

Existing landlords are "between a rock and a hard place," says William Robert, a Scottsdale, Ariz.-based former banker who now invests in distressed property with his sons. "They can't make their debt service on the lower rental rates that are available."

Special servicers, the troubled loan specialists that oversee hundreds of billions in commercial mortgages on behalf of bond investors, have sold $11.6 billion in distressed loans and foreclosed property in the past 12 months, according to analysts at Deutsche Bank AG.

That's up from $7 billion over the prior 12-month period and from $1.2 billion in the 12 months prior to that, according to Deutsche Bank.

By and large, these assets are being shed at big losses. Of the loans sold by the special servicers, bond investors suffered an average 38% loss on loans or property sold this year, according to Deutsche Bank.

"For the first year or two of the crisis, stuff was coming in faster than it would go out," said Tad Philipp, a managing director at Moody's Investor Service who tracks commercial mortgages. Now, he said, "a whole lot of stuff that got into trouble in '09 has now gone through the process."

While bank asset sales statistics aren't available, some banks also have picked up the pace of sales activity, say buyers of distressed properties.

"They're finally starting to dump these assets at prices that make sense," said Nate Paul, chief executive of World Class Capital Group, an Austin, Texas-based private-equity firm that has spent about $150 million on 25 acquisitions this year.

Distressed property sales have been occurring throughout the economic recovery. But the commercial mortgage sales throughout 2009 and 2010 were generally dominated by a few banks and the Federal Deposit Insurance Corp., which unloaded assets to major private-equity firms in large pools, sometimes filled with hundreds of properties.

Now the workload for lenders and servicers is growing, as troubled loans made during the boom years increasingly come due.

Around $400 billion of commercial real-estate loans made by all lenders mature in 2012, up from $375 billion this year and $250 billion in 2010, according to Deutsche Bank.

To be sure, many property owners who are unable to pay off mortgages amid a sluggish economic recovery are cutting deals with lenders and servicers to modify and extend the terms of these loans.

But each month, billions of dollars of new properties fall into default.

The delinquency rate for loans tied to commercial mortgage-backed securities shot up through 2009 and 2010 and has hovered between 9% and 10% for much of the past year. The rate reached 9.8% in October, its second-highest mark ever, according to real estate research service Trepp LLC.

"What you're going to see over the next three years is a potential ever-increasing volume of loans going into default," said Robert Lieber, executive managing director of C-III Capital Partners LLC, which controls a special servicer that currently oversees more than $17 billion in troubled commercial loans and foreclosed properties.

Buyers are welcoming the increased volume of distressed assets being put on the block.

Many of them raised money in the early years of the downturn in hopes of buying discounted properties but until recently there hasn't been much for sale.

Opportunities are increasing in particular for small investors like Mr. Nguyen. Most of the loans and properties being sold by special servicers are small, with an average size in 2011 of $8.4 million, according to Deutsche Bank.

Auction.com, a commercial loan-sale website that has sold more than $5 billion in commercial loans and foreclosed properties this year, says 80% to 90% of its buyers are smaller local investors.

US
Tax Win Inspires Copycats; Lake Tahoe Homeowners Who Won Record $43 Million Refund Seek Even More
By Jim Carlton
840 words
1 November 2011
The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
English
Copyright 2011 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.

INCLINE VILLAGE, Nev.One of the country's most successful property tax revolts is about to get another day in court, as lawyers for residents in this wealthy Lake Tahoe community who won a record $43 million in refunds are seeking even more.

The residents argue that Washoe County's assessments should be "equalized" with those of a nearby county, which has comparable homes abutting the lake but assessments up to 50% lower. Lawyers for the residents will press the Nevada Supreme Court on Tuesday to reinstate a case that argues such equalization should take place.

Among the Incline Village homeowners entitled to refunds under the $43 million ruling in July are Oracle Corp. billionaire Larry Ellison, former junk-bond king Michael Milken and Peoplesoft founder Dave Duffield.

U.S. homeowners increasingly are challenging tax assessments. In some cases, they are contesting run-ups in property assessments when the housing market was flush. In other cases, homeowners argue their assessments haven't fallen as much as their home values have since 2008.

In Toms River, N.J., about 1,000 homeowners won refunds totaling over $1 million after filing appeals of their township assessments from 2009 to 2011, when they saw their house values fall faster than their assessments, said Philip Giannuario, an attorney representing them.

The Nevada lawsuit could encourage other regions to fight property taxes, said Sheila Anderson, a broker at Commercial Property Services Inc. in Ocala, Fla., who helped organize residential property-tax protests in her state. "I think it's very significant and we're all watching it," she said.

In Sandpoint, Idaho, members of an antitax group say the Nevada fight inspired them to take actions including voting in county commissioners more amenable to reining in property tax increases.

The Tahoe case also led to passage of a 2005 Nevada law that took away the power of counties to use their own methodologies to set tax assessments.

Tax experts say how the Nevada case plays out could have more repercussions nationally. "Any time residential taxpayers can see somebody be hugely successful in a tax appeal, of course it inspires them," said John Garippa, president of American Property Tax Counsel, a Chicago-based trade group for property-tax attorneys.

If the Nevada Supreme Court rules in the Tahoe residents' favor, and the lower court that turned away the case follows suit, residents could receive millions more in refunds on top of those the court awarded in July, their lawyers said. The court ruled then that Washoe County improperly raised property taxes on 8,700 homeowners in Incline Village and adjoining Crystal Bay.

The $43 million refund award is believed to be the highest ever in the U.S., Mr. Garippa said.

The Nevada dispute began after homeowners saw property taxes jump in 2003. Kern Schumacher said the tax bill for his 12,900-square-foot house in Incline Village rose 40% to $200,539 after a rise of less than 1% in 2002. Although he said he could afford the increase, the railroad executive banded with neighbors against it.

The attitude [of county officials] is we have more money than everyone else and we can afford it," said the 71-year-old Mr. Schumacher, whose house is valued by the county at $11.2 million.

Washoe County officials blame a 1981 change in state tax law that let county assessors use their own techniques to value homes rather than relying strictly on market values. "It kind of put all county assessors in the state in a bit of a no-man's land," said David Creekman, Washoe County's chief deputy district attorney, who is helping defend the county in the case.

Mr. Creekman said soaring property values prompted double-digit tax increases on the Incline Village area in 2003 using methodologies that placed a premium on lake views and beaches. "Obviously, at Incline Village, the view and type of lake frontage plays a significant value in the property," said Josh Wilson, the current assessor.

But some homeowners considered the process unfair. Maryanne Ingemanson, 78, a retired concert pianist and developer, helped form the Nevada Property Tax Revolt, which filed multiple lawsuits challenging the tax system. From 2003 to 2006, the Nevada Supreme Court awarded $6.1 million in refunds to 1,200 homeowners who filed appeals.

In 2010, the Incline Village group filed a suit in state district court that led to the Supreme Court's ruling ordering the $43 million in refunds. That award put an added strain on a county that has laid off a quarter of its work force since 2007 because of the weak economy.

County officials declined to estimate how much more in tax-refund liability they could face in the case being heard Tuesday. "It's something that will impact us," said John Sherman, Washoe County's finance director.

Opinion
Romney's Finest Hour; He speaks the truth about housing and foreclosures.
965 words
28 October 2011
The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
11
English
Copyright 2011 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.

A friend of ours quipped recently that Mitt Romney could do his Presidential candidacy a lot of good if he took even a single position that is unpopular in the polls. Well, we can report that he has done that on housing policy, that he's being pummeled for it, and that it may be his finest campaign hour. It also contrasts favorably with the latest temporary, ad hoc and futile housing effort from President Obama.

Campaigning last week in Nevada, the epicenter of the housing bust, Mr. Romney was asked by the Las Vegas Review-Journal editorial board what he would do about housing and foreclosures. His reply:

"One is, don't try and stop the foreclosure process. Let it run its course and hit the bottom. Allow investors to buy homes, put renters in them, fix the homes up. Let it turn around and come back up. The Obama Administration has slow-walked the foreclosure processes that have long existed, and as a result we still have a foreclosure overhang."

How's that for refreshing? After five years of politicians trying without success to levitate the housing market by postponing foreclosures, Mr. Romney dared to tell the truth. Parts of the U.S., including Nevada, still have too many homes, and that supply needs to be sold off and fixed up so the market can find a bottom before home prices can start to rise again. The faster that process proceeds, the faster the recovery will take hold.

For this apostasy, Mr. Romney is getting whacked by the Democratic National Committee in a 30-second TV ad that first aired Tuesday in Arizona: "Almost half of Arizona homeowners underwater. Foreclosures everywhere. And what's Mitt Romney's plan?" the ad intones. Then it quotes Mr. Romney: "Don't try and stop the foreclosure process. Let it run its course and hit the bottom.'"

The attack ad doesn't quote the second part of Mr. Romney's Las Vegas answer, which spoke another truth: "Number two, the credit [that] was given to first time homebuyers was insufficient and inadequate to turn around the housing market. I think it was an ineffective idea. It was a little bit like the cash-for-clunkers program, throwing government money at something which was not market-oriented, did not staunch the decline in home values anymore than it encouraged the auto industry to take off."

While he was at it, Mr. Romney might have added to his list of "ineffective" housing ideas the Bush Administration's Hope Now program, the Barney Frank-George W. Bush Hope for Homeowners plan, the Obama Administration Home Affordable Modification Program, the Emergency Homeowners' Loan Program, and, this week, an expanded version of the March 2009 Home Affordable Refinance Program (Harp).

All of these government plans used taxpayer cash to forestall foreclosures in an attempt to stop housing prices from falling from their manic heights. How's that working out? Five years into the housing bust, the U.S. still has 10.9 million "underwater" borrowers, whose homes are worth less than the original purchase price. States like Florida, Nevada and New Jersey have long foreclosure backlogs, and home prices still haven't begun to recover in much of the country.

Mr. Romney's advice to let the foreclosure and resale process take its course as rapidly as possible until the market finds a "bottom" couldn't possibly do any worse than the Obama Administration and its frenetic attempts to "save" homeowners have done. To the extent that it encourages a faster recovery it is also more compassionate. As the nearby chart shows, while the fall in home prices has been painful for current owners, it has also made housing far more affordable for new buyers.

Which brings us to the latest White House housing gesture, Harp II, to make it easier to refinance mortgages guaranteed by Fannie Mae and Freddie Mac. Harp I was supposed to help four to five million borrowers refinance, but only around 894,000 have used it so far.

So why not double down on lack of success? Harp II will remove many of the restrictions on banks and borrowers that were contained in Harp I to protect taxpayers. These include removing the 125% loan-to-value ceiling for underwater borrowers, dealing with bank litigation concerns, and eliminating the need for a new appraisal. It also extends the end of the program to December 31, 2013, from next June 30.

No doubt this will help some homeowners refinance, especially with the Federal Reserve continuing to push long-bond rates even lower. But it is not a free lunch, especially for investors who hold mortgage-backed securities. Those securities will fall in value as borrowers prepay their old loans, and sure enough the MBS market fell out of bed after the White House announcement on Monday.

The Congressional Budget Office tested an economic model of the mock refinancing plan in September and estimated that while government enterprises like Fannie and Freddie would save $3.9 billion from refinancing, they'd also lose $4.5 billion from the reduced value of their mortgage-backed securities. Pension funds, banks and others would lose as much as $15 billion. Such investors don't get any sympathy these days, but don't be surprised if they're skittish about re-entering America's politicized housing market in the future.

As for Mr. Romney, in his Vegas interview he did add briefly that "the idea of helping people refinance homes to stay in them is one that's worth further consideration," subject to more information. That's the poll-driven candidate talking, but for today let's congratulate the truth-teller.

Markets
Mortgage Bond Investors Seek 'Obama Button' to Divine Trades
By Al Yoon
774 words
28 October 2011
11:37
The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
English
Copyright 2011 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.

NEW YORKInvesting in the $5 trillion agency mortgage-backed securities market has long been a technician's game, with statistics and mathematical formulas frequently guiding investor decisions.

But the last few years have seen greater uncertainty in predicting key variablesin particular how fast homeowners will repay their loansbecause federal policy makers have repeatedly changed the rules of the mortgage game via the Obama administration's ad hoc remedies to housing-market woes.

This has led dealers to retool, and investors to rethink, the mathematical models that for decades have sought to identify the most profitable bonds and warn against clunkers.

FTN Financial mortgage strategist Walter Schmidt said an investor joked to him that one way to improve models would be to add an "Obama button" that could magically factor in the effect of White House policy changes wrought through the government's control of housing-finance giants Fannie Mae and Freddie Mac.

"It's been an extremely challenging time to model" with policy changes, said Dale Westhoff, global head of structured product research at Credit Suisse in New York. In years past, there's been "nothing close" to the challenge faced by modelers today, he said.

A 21-year veteran of such models, he's not giving up, however. Credit Suisse's seven-man modeling team is developing a second model that seeks to anticipate the effects of the Home Affordable Refinance Program, which aims to help about one million borrowers get new, cheaper loans by eliminating the home value from the mortgage approval process and easing liabilities banks face in extending such credit.

The right to refinance is common to most mortgages but is the primary risk in agency-issued mortgage-backed securities. Those bonds, issued by Fannie Mae and Freddie Mac, support most of the U.S. home loan market.

Mortgage securities issued two or more years ago come with coupons, or nominal interest rates, far above what investors can earn today. To get them, some investors have paid more than the face value of the security. When the underlying loans are refinanced, however, they are paid back at par, or face value, which results in a loss to bondholders.

Getting prepayment estimates wrong can be costly. For a typical portfolio of a real-estate investment trust, net margins on the 30-year, 4.5% mortgage security from Fannie Mae would fall to 1.21% from nearly 2% if the constant prepayment ratea measure of mortgage prepayments as a percentage of the current outstanding loan balancerose to 30% from 20%, according to a study by investor American Capital Agency Corp.

A separate Obama program to let students with government education loans refinance also set analysts into action this week because it could help some asset-backed securities and hurt others.

A measure of investor concern about faster home mortgage refinancing came this week as 300 investors listened in on a Credit Suisse conference call about HARP changes, more than usual, said Mr. Westhoff. Analysts are working "double-time" to get their new model up and running on Monday, he added.

Barclays Capital detailed its new model on Friday.

Some investors said they might watch models but they haven't trusted the output, even before the more prominent role of government intervention.

A September study by Pacific Investment Management Co., whose Total Return Fund in September held $92 billion of mortgage securities, found estimates on option-adjusted spreads of Fannie Mae MBS of nine Wall Street firms varied as much as 0.76 percentage point from one another.

Of the last 45 changes in some Wall Street models, 43 made the MBS appear cheaper, suggesting the changes are made to make bonds more attractive to investors, said Scott Simon, head of Pimco's mortgage- and asset-backed securities teams in Newport Beach, Calif.

"You've got to acknowledge that what might look best in the model is not always where you want to take risk in your portfolio," said Tim Cunneen, a senior portfolio manager at Smith Breeden Associates in Durham, N.C.

Models are getting better all the time, though, said Credit Suisse's Mr. Westhoff. Collecting data through the worst housing bust since the 1930s means analysts now have the benefit of information through periods where home prices were rising and refinancing was easy, and also when property values are falling and credit is lacking, he said.

"I am thoroughly convinced that on the other side of this we are going to have better models than we've ever had before," he said.

WSJO
English
Copyright 2011 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.

Fixed mortgage rates changed little over the past week after reports on the health of the U.S. economy showed mixed signals, according to Freddie Mac's weekly survey.

"The latest monthly housing market indicators were mixed, with consumer confidence soft, house prices largely flat, and new home sales up from very low levels," said Freddie Mac Chief Economist Frank Nothaft.

The 30-year fixed-rate mortgage averaged 4.10% for the week ended Thursday, down slightly from 4.11% the previous week and below 4.23% recorded last year. Rates on 15-year fixed-rate mortgages averaged 3.38%, unchanged from last week and below 3.66% a year ago.

Five-year Treasury-indexed hybrid adjustable-rate mortgages averaged 3.08%, up from 3.01% last week and below 3.41% a year ago. One-year Treasury-indexed ARM rates averaged 2.90%, down from 2.94% in the prior week and 3.3% last year.

Even as much of the nation's housing market remains tough, Silicon Valley's property scene has been relatively buoyant. That is apparent even in the highest end of the market in the exclusive town of Atherton, where numerous venture capitalists and top technology executives such as Hewlett-Packard Co. chief executive Meg Whitman have homes.

Overall, the median price of a home in Atherton was $3.2 million from January to August of this year, up 10.3% from $2.9 million in the same period a year ago, according to DataQuick. The town's median home price rose even though the number of property transactions67 dealsstayed the same this year versus the same period last year, says DataQuick.

In contrast, the median sale price of a home in all of California over the same period fell 5.7% to $250,000 from $265,000, according to DataQuick.

Among the home buyers in Atherton this year was Jeffrey Housenbold, CEO of online photo company Shutterfly Inc., who purchased a $10.4 million property in May, according to public records. Google Inc. engineering director Kourosh Gharachorloo also bought an Atherton home, shelling out $8.6 million in February, according to public records.

Representatives for Shutterfly and Google declined to comment on the home purchases.

Local real-estate agents say the strong demand for properties in Athertonwhich is centrally located and typically boasts relatively spacious one-acre lots for homeshas prompted a rush of builders to target new development in the town.

Wai H. Yip, a local builder, said he faced plenty of competition from other developers over an Atherton lot and house that he bought last year for around $3 million. Since then, he has torn down the house and is constructing a nearly 12,000-square-foot new one, which he aims to put on the market in 2012 for more than double what he paid.

"We've already gotten [buyer] interest," Mr. Yip said. "The market is going strong."

But not all techie buyers are flocking to Atherton, said Silicon Valley real-estate agent Ken DeLeon of Keller Williams Realty. In particular, Mr. DeLeon said he has been working with young employees from companies including Facebook Inc. and that many of them are shying away from Atherton in favor of hipper places like Palo Alto.

"They're less inclined to go to Atherton than successful CEOs and executives," said Mr. DeLeon. "They have a sense that Atherton is stuffy."

Business Orders, Home Sales Tick Up
By Josh Mitchell and Sara Murray
567 words
27 October 2011
The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
English
Copyright 2011 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.

Businesses are stepping up investment, even as their overall orders for big-ticket items such as automobiles and aircraft dropped last month, new data show.

A separate report offered a hint of optimism for the battered housing market as new-home sales ticked up 5.7% in September, the first increase in five months, the Commerce Department said Wednesday. New home sales are still on pace for the worst year on record.

New orders for durable goodsthose designed to last longer than three yearsfell by 0.8% in September to $200.3 billion, the Commerce Department said. But the decline was largely because of a slump in demand for airplanes, a category that tends to shift drastically from month to month even in a healthy economy.

Excluding transportation, the report was more promising: New orders rose 1.7%. Demand for nondefense-related capital goods excluding aircraftan indicator of business investmentjumped 2.4%. Computers, electrical equipment and metals all showed strong gains. Shipments fell 0.7% last month after four months of increases.

The rise in orders suggests companies are continuing to invest their cash despite a weak recovery, budget wars in Washington and economic turmoil in Europe.

Ian Shepherdson, chief U.S. economist at High Frequency Economics Ltd., said big companies are using substantial cash reserves to replace equipment but not necessarily to expand. "There's a sort of capital arms race, even if it's not an arms race toward expansion, but an arms race to keep up with the best of what's available. If you don't, you lose market share," Mr. Shepherdson said.

Business has picked up at Paccar Inc., a Bellevue, Wash., commercial-truck maker, which saw third-quarter earnings more than double, the company said Tuesday. Sales in the U.S. and Canada rose 80% from a year earlier as more customers replaced aging fleets.

"It's been an up-and-down decade for many of them, but those that have survived are well positioned," Chief Executive Mark Pigott said in a conference call Tuesday.

Europe's economic turmoil increases uncertainty for manufactures such as Paccar, which is ratcheting back its European production. Still, the company has expansion plans in the works and estimates it will spend between $450 million and $550 million on capital projects in 2012.

The housing market, which has notched some minor improvements, continues to weigh on the recovery. Last month's increase in new-home sales was the strongest pace since April. But prices kept falling, and the new seasonally adjusted annual rate of 313,000 units sold puts the market on pace for the worst sales year since record-keeping began in 1963.

New-home sales remain about a quarter of their peak before the housing bubble started deflating around five years ago. Sales are far below healthy levels, considered to be an annual rate of around 750,000, and are still 0.9% below year-ago levels.

In another bid to stabilize the sector, the Obama administration and a U.S. housing regulator this week unveiled a plan to let borrowers refinance regardless of how far their homes have fallen in value.

Jeff Bater contributed to this article.

After lingering on the market for a year, a newly renovated beach house in Sagaponack owned by Scott N. Resnick, a scion of the Resnick real-estate family, sold last month for $24 million, $6 million more than he paid for it in 2007 near the peak of the market.

That sale is part of a rebound in pricier Hamptons homes that began in the spring and continued into the fall, despite the challenging larger environment for sales across the region. Median prices fell compared with the second quarter, but were far above levels reported in the same quarter in 2010, according to reports released Thursday.

"There is a lot going on in the high end, someone's got to be making money, said John Healey, a broker at Town & Country Real Estate who listed Mr. Resnick's modern seven-bedroom home with pool. That sale was the most expensive closed sale in the third quarter, reported to date.

He attributed much of the increase in price for Mr. Resnick's home at 137 Crestview Lane, on a major renovation that took so long that the sellers outgrew the space. Mr. Resnick didn't return a request for comment.

The median sales price in the Hamptons was $850,000, according to a report by Prudential Douglas Elliman, down 9.3% from the second quarter, but up 22.1% from the same period in 2010, a particularly weak quarter.

Jonathan Miller, an appraiser and president of Miller Samuel, said that there were 30 sales reported for more than $5 million in the third quarter, the second highest total since the financial crisis in the fall of 2008. But taking out the luxury boomlet, prices in the Hamptons and across Long Island were at best essentially stable, with "a modest uptick in activity," he said.

Cia Comnas, who manages East End offices for Brown Harris Stevens, said that despite a "return of investor confidence in the high-end Hampton properties," the market remains challenging.

Despite the overall increase in sales compared to a year earlier, a report by Brown Harris Stevens flagged the relative weakness in lower priced apartments. It found that homes that sold for less than $1 million made up 57% of the market in the third quarter compared to 67% in the same quarter, as sales of more expensive apartments rose.

Analysts noted that the quarterly shifts in sales and prices were also influenced by federal policy, the expiration of federal tax credits for home purchases and a rush to sell expensive properties last year, out of a fear that the federal capital gains tax rate would rise.

A number of other top sales in the third quarter were for large houses fronting or near the beach in Southampton Village and in Sagaponack.

Susan Breichtenbach, a broker with the Corcoran Group in Bridgehampton, said that while the inventory of luxury houses remains high in the Hamptons, there was a strong demand for unusual oceanfront properties, if they were priced right. "There is some money out there," she said.

Real Estate
Apartment Values Rise, as Do Rents
By Dawn Wotapka
905 words
26 October 2011
The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
English
Copyright 2011 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.

Strong growth of rents and occupancy levels of rental apartments have pushed some building values to record levels as Americans shift away from home ownership.

While concerns about the economy are cooling the market for most other types of commercial real estate, apartment rents and occupancies continue to be boosted by demand from millions of people who are victims of foreclosure or are unwilling or unable to buy their own homes.

At the end of the third quarter, 5.6% of the nation's apartments were vacant, down from 5.9% in the second quarter, and the lowest level since 2006, according to Reis Inc., a real-estate data service.

Rents are up even in some cities that have been hard hit by high unemployment and the housing crash, like Orlando, Fla., Detroit and Phoenix. Effective rents, which include landlord discounts in some markets, rose to $1,004 a month in the third quarter, up 2.3% from a year earlier, according to Reis. Of the 82 major markets that Reis tracks, only Las Vegas saw rents decline compared with a year earlier.

Forecasters say rent increases could slow or stop if the economy weakens further. But for now, these trends are producing outsized returns for real-estate companies, compared with other commercial-property classes.

Values of apartment buildings in the best locationswith modern amenities like resort-style swimming pools and outdoor movie viewing areaswent into record territory in the third quarter, according to an index compiled by Green Street Advisors. The previous record had been set in the second quarter of 2007.

Investors who bought apartment buildings just a few years ago are selling for big profits. Regency Club, a 372-unit complex in Jackson, N.J., with two swimming pools and tennis courts, sold for $44 million in August, compared with $39.9 million in early 2009, according to Marcus & Millichap.

At the same time, though, the rise in rents is squeezing large swathes of the middle class by increasing living costs just as wage increases are anemic and unemployment high.

Glen Guile, a 40-year-old information technology and marketing employee for an auto-parts company in Raleigh, N.C., says he's looking on Craigslist, an online classified-ad service, for a roommate because he just heard his $629 rent for a one-bedroom apartment could be increased another $30 to $40 a month. He's already working a second job at a Costco store. "I don't get a day off. I work seven days a week," he said.

But thanks to rising rents and occupancies, some analysts predict that real-estate companies will have the highest growth in property net income this year and next year since 2006.

Associated Estates Realty Corp. kicked off the earnings season for apartment-building companies Monday by reporting a 12.5% year-over-year increase in funds from operations, a common metric used by real-estate companies to measure performance. When looking at apartments owned for a year or more, rents for Associated's 12,000-unit portfolio were up 4.6% compared with the third quarter of 2010.

"Some people try to make the argument that what's going on in the job market affects apartment demand," said Jeffrey Friedman, Associated's chief executive. "We don't believe that."

The apartment sector has been insulated from high unemployment because it continues to inhabit a sweet spot in the economy created by demographic factors and the anemic home sales market. The U.S. is expected to see 1.5 million rental household formations in 2011, a record year, according to Green Street.

The main reason for the rental increase is a faster-than-expected decline in the home ownership rate, according to Green Street. The nation's rate came in at 66% in the second quarter, down from 66.4% in the first quarter and 66.9% in the second quarter a year ago, according to the Census Bureau.

Some industry watchers say the rate could fall to as low as 60%. Each 1% decline in the home-ownership rate represents the movement of one million households to rentals.

If a current tenant balks about a lease renewal including higher rent, Mr. Friedman says he isn't overly concerned. "There's someone coming right behind them who can afford it," he said.

To be sure, the economics of apartment investments aren't detached from the concerns about financial problems in Europe and the possibility of a double-dip recession in the U.S. As a result, landlords have started to temper rent growth in some areas, including Denver, Atlanta and the Baltimore area, according to Green Street.

If another recession hits and unemployment rises, millions of renters could likely double up or move home with their parents, putting a crimp in demand. "People just aren't going to write bigger and bigger rent checks into infinity," warns Andrew McCulloch a Green Street analyst.

The high rents are also being supported by a lack of new supply. Developers have scrambled to launch new projects, but most of them won't start hitting the market until late 2012. Roughly 8,200 new apartments hit the market in the third quarter, the second lowest number since Reis began tracking data in 1999.

Home Prices Show Sector Still Ailing
By S. Mitra Kalita

The Federal Housing Finance Agency said home prices slipped 0.1% on a seasonally adjusted basis, and 4% over the prior year; the index is calculated from the purchase prices of homes backed by mortgages sold to or guaranteed by Fannie Mae or Freddie Mac.

Together, the home-price measures drew a pessimistic reaction from industry watchers. "We believe national home prices have further to fall," a Bank of America Merrill Lynch Global Research report said. "The continued decline in home prices is a function of the large imbalance between supply and demand in the housing market. There is too much inventory, particularly of distressed properties, relative to the pace of home sales, which means that prices must fall further to equilibrate the market."

Indeed, in the Case-Shiller index, Detroit and Washington were the only cities to post annual growth; the Midwestspecifically Chicago, Detroit and Minneapolisshowed sharp monthly increases. Economists attribute this to some pickup in hiring in the automotive sector. In Chicago, first-time and luxury buyers are jump-starting the market, says Mark Pullinger, a spokesman for Koenig & Strey Real Living, a large brokerage in the area. "Inventory is down substantially, so it's being driven bybargain hunters," he said. "There are areas that if you ever wanted to live in, now is the time to buy because the prices are relatively low. The people in the middle are a little bit stuck. Their homes are not worth what they paid for them recently, so it's tough to move up."

The Case-Shiller data come on the heels of the White House's revamp of a mortgage-refinance program for "underwater" borrowersthose who owe more than their homes are worth. But economists say there are few quick fixes for the housing crisis, and easier refinancing rules will do little to address weak demandfor homes.

"It was a very bad spring-to-summer-market season," saidNancy Wallace, a finance professor at the University of California at Berkeley. She, said a turnaround in the housingmarket remains largely dependent on a loosening of credit and a surge in hiring. "People are almost afraid to apply for mortgages and lots of people have little scratches and dents on their credit right now," she said.

U.S. News: Obama Expands Mortgage Aid --- Revamped Refinancing Plan Seen as Help but Not Cure for Weak Housing Market
By Nick Timiraos
657 words
25 October 2011
The Wall Street Journal
J
A4
English
(Copyright (c) 2011, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)

The federal government's revamped mortgage-refinancing program could allow more than a million borrowers to take advantage of falling interest rates, even if the value of their homes has plummeted.

While the plan, announced as expected on Monday, could provide a modest boost to spending and some relief to homeowners, economists said more action may be needed to stabilize the ailing housing market.

The overhaul of the Home Affordable Refinance Program, or HARP, will let borrowers whose mortgages are backed by Fannie Mae and Freddie Mac refinance, regardless of how far their homes' values have fallen, eliminating a previous limit.

"If you meet certain requirements, you will have the chance to refinance at lower rates, which could save you hundreds of dollars a month, and thousands of dollars a year in mortgage payments," President Barack Obama said during a speech Monday in Las Vegas.

Monday's changes will allow borrowers to refinance their mortgages regardless of how far home prices have plunged in any given market. Loans that exceed the current 125% loan-to-value limit won't be eligible to refinance until early next year, officials said.

Other homeowners will still be left out, including those whose mortgages aren't guaranteed by Fannie and Freddie and those who took out mortgages in the past 2 1/2 years.

Mr. Obama unveiled HARP one month into his presidency. But so far, only 894,000 borrowers have used it, of which just 70,000 are significantly underwater, or owe more than their homes are worth.

The ultimate success of the effort depends on how lenders handle a batch of policy revisions, some of which have yet to be spelled out. While the changes announced Monday are a "very positive step, it isn't a magic bullet," said Mark Zandi, chief economist at Moody's Analytics. "Policymakers will thus very likely have to do more to support the housing market."

Some critics have warned of unintended consequences to mortgage markets if investors worry that political intervention will lead mortgage-backed securities to pay off sooner than expected as loans refinance, leaving investors with cash to invest at lower rates.

Rep. Spencer Bachus (R., Ala.), the chairman of the House Financial Services Committee, criticized the refinance plan on Monday. "This is another example of the administration picking winners and losers," he said.

The policy tweaks won't do much to improve the equity position of about 11 million borrowers who are underwater, and they won't help boost underlying housing demand, which remains depressed.

New York Fed President William Dudley said the changes were "a step in the right direction" but said a "comprehensive approach" was needed to stabilize home prices.

Analysts said one of the most important changes in the refinance rules concerned "buy-backs," where banks are forced to repurchase from Fannie and Freddie defaulted loans with underwriting flaws. That has discouraged many lenders from refinancing all but the safest loans.

Banks "will sit on the sidelines as long as they fear" that refinancing will put them at risk, said Gene Sperling, the president's top economic adviser.

The changes should help borrowers like James Sims of Fairfield, Ohio, who has a Fannie-guaranteed mortgage with a 6.16% rate. He meets the guidelines of the HARP program, but because he has mortgage insurance, he had been told earlier he had to refinance with the company that services his mortgage.

That's a problem because the servicing company, Seterus Inc., a unit of International Business Machines Corp., doesn't make loans. "I've just kind of given up on it," Mr. Sims said. Seterus declined to comment. Under the new changes, Mr. Sims should now be able to go to any lender to refinance his mortgage.

Obama Expands Mortgage Aid; Revamped Refinancing Plan Seen as Help but Not Cure for Weak Housing Market
By Nick Timiraos
940 words
25 October 2011
The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
English
Copyright 2011 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.

The federal government's revamped mortgage-refinancing program could allow more than a million borrowers to take advantage of falling interest rates, even if the value of their homes has plummeted.

While the plan, announced as expected on Monday, could provide a modest boost to spending and some relief to homeowners, economists said more action may be needed to stabilize the ailing housing market.

The overhaul of the Home Affordable Refinance Program, or HARP, will let borrowers whose mortgages are backed by Fannie Mae and Freddie Mac refinance, regardless of how far their homes' values have fallen, eliminating a previous limit.

"If you meet certain requirements, you will have the chance to refinance at lower rates, which could save you hundreds of dollars a month, and thousands of dollars a year in mortgage payments," President Barack Obama said during a speech Monday in Las Vegas.

The Obama administration has been criticized for housing-relief programs that failed to meet announced goals, and declined Monday to provide estimates of how many borrowers might be helped by the latest effort. Analysts at Barclays Capital estimate that between two million and three million borrowers could be eligible under the revamp.

Monday's changes will allow borrowers to refinance their mortgages regardless of how far home prices have plunged in any given market. Loans that exceed the current 125% loan-to-value limit won't be eligible to refinance until early next year, officials said.

Other homeowners will still be left out, including those whose mortgages aren't guaranteed by Fannie and Freddie and those who took out mortgages in the past 2 years.

Mr. Obama unveiled HARP one month into his presidency. But so far, only 894,000 borrowers have used it, of which just 70,000 are significantly underwater, or owe more than their homes are worth.

The ultimate success of the effort depends on how lenders handle a batch of policy revisions, some of which have yet to be spelled out. While the changes announced Monday are a "very positive step, it isn't a magic bullet," said Mark Zandi, chief economist at Moody's Analytics. "Policymakers will thus very likely have to do more to support the housing market."

Some critics have warned of unintended consequences to mortgage markets if investors worry that political intervention will lead mortgage-backed securities to pay off sooner than expected as loans refinance, leaving investors with cash to invest at lower rates.

Rep. Spencer Bachus (R., Ala.), the chairman of the House Financial Services Committee, criticized the refinance plan on Monday. "This is another example of the administration picking winners and losers," he said.

The policy tweaks won't do much to improve the equity position of about 11 million borrowers who are underwater, and they won't help boost underlying housing demand, which remains depressed.

New York Fed President William Dudley said the changes were "a step in the right direction" but said a "comprehensive approach" was needed to stabilize home prices.Mr. Dudley is one of several Fed officials who in recent days have called for more urgent action to help housing. Fed Governor Daniel Tarullo last week urged the central bank to resume large-scale purchases of mortgage-backed securities to drive mortgage rates even lower.

Analysts said one of the most important changes in the refinance rules Monday concerned "buy-backs," where banks are forced to repurchase from Fannie and Freddie defaulted loans with underwriting flaws. That has discouraged many lenders from refinancing all but the safest loans.

Banks "will sit on the sidelines as long as they fear" that refinancing will put them at risk, said Gene Sperling, the president's top economic adviser. Mr. Sperling said the decision by the Federal Housing Finance Agency, which regulates Fannie and Freddie, to offer a substantial waiver from potential put-backs on HARP loans created the potential to "unleash" more refinancing. On Monday, call volume at the Bank of America mortgage offices was "higher than usual," a spokeswoman said, and the bank was taking steps to put special teams in place "to respond to greater volume in the days ahead."

The changes should help borrowers like James Sims of Fairfield, Ohio, who has a Fannie-guaranteed mortgage with a 6.16% rate. He meets the guidelines of the HARP program but because he has mortgage insurance, he had been told earlier he had to refinance with the company that services his mortgage.

That's a problem because the servicing company, Seterus Inc., a unit of International Business Machines Corp, doesn't make loans. "I've just kind of given up on it," Mr. Sims said. Seterus declined to comment. Under the new changes, Mr. Sims should now be able to go to any lender to refinance his mortgage.

The change should help many borrowers in the hardest-hit housing markets. Ronald Gallagher's daughter, a single mother of two and a nurse, made a 25% down payment when she bought her Phoenix house five years ago. Today she owes around 50% more than the property's estimated value of $142,000, leaving her too far underwater to refinance, under earlier rules.

Her mortgage, which is backed by Freddie, will reset next year to a variable rate. If she were able to refinance, "I can guarantee you ... she'd be buying stuff that she isn't able to buy right now," such as new carpet for her home, said Mr. Gallagher.

Plan Highlights Sharp Political Split
By Jonathan Weisman And Patrick O'Connor
945 words
25 October 2011
The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
English
Copyright 2011 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.

President Barack Obama on Monday went where his Republican White House rivals have so far refused to go. He asserted that Washington should help Americans refinance their mortgages at lower rates.

The president's move to expand an existing, little-used program underscored his administration's belief that government has a role to play in restoring the health of the nation's broken housing market. In contrast, Republican presidential hopefuls have been reluctant to address the housing issue, in part because they blame government for causing the financial crisis and housing mess.

In 2008, Republican presidential candidate John McCain proposed that the government buy up home mortgages that exceeded the value of houses, then re-issue them at market value. "He got killed," said Douglas Holtz-Eakin, the economic adviser who had urged Mr. McCain to make the proposal.

Months later, the tea-party movement took off after CNBC analyst Rick Santelli's on-air tirade in February 2009 about the new Obama administration's suggestion that it would try to aid homeowners. "How many of you people want to pay for your neighbor's mortgage that has an extra bathroom and can't pay their bills?" he asked.

Ever since, politicians from both parties have feared aggressive action that would smack of welfare for McMansion dwellers.

But in economic circles, calls for a more aggressive approach to the housing crisis are bipartisan.

The president's latest refinancing plan was based in part on a proposal by R. Glenn Hubbard, Columbia University's business-school dean and a senior economic adviser to Republican front-runner Mitt Romney.

If the president's effort "is the mass refinancing we suggest, it could be a very big deal," Mr. Hubbard said in an interview Monday.

Georgia Republican Sen. Johnny Isakson, who has a similar proposal, called the Obama plan "a no-brainer."

Harvard University economist Martin Feldstein, a senior adviser to Ronald Reagan, called for more aggressive government action earlier this month. The government, he said, should reduce the principal amount owed on mortgages to no more than 110% of the value of a house.

Mr. Romney, meeting with the Las Vegas Review Journal last week, took a different approach. "Don't try and stop the foreclosure process. Let it run its course and hit the bottom, allow investors to buy homes, put renters in them, fix the homes up, and let it turn around," Mr. Romney counseled.

Mr. Romney said the Obama administration's efforts to that point had only "slow-walked" the foreclosure process and left an "overhang" of homeowners treading water with mortgages worth more than their homes, waiting for a rescue.

These may have been the most detailed Republican comments on housing so far in the 2012 race.

Mr. Romney's 59-point economic plan didn't include a point on housing. Instead, Republican candidates have tended to criticize government-sponsored enterprises Fannie Mae and Freddie Mac for fueling the housing bubble by facilitating home loans made to people with shaky credit. And many congressional Republicans have said that Mr. Obama's previous housing programs have failed to help the housing market in any meaningful way.

The housing issue hasn't come up much during GOP presidential debates. In one instance when it did, Texas Rep. Ron Paul said the government should stand aside and let home prices continue to fall until the existing property becomes a good value.

Two candidates who have offered plans to address the housing crisisMinnesota Rep. Michele Bachmann and former Utah Gov. Jon Huntsman Jr.call for privatizing Fannie Mae and Freddie Mac, which hold an estimated 70% of home loans in the U.S., and were taken over by the government during the 2008 crisis.

Mortgage- and housing-industry lobbyists warn that removing the government backstop is unrealistic and could send mortgage rates soaring. And bills to transform Fannie and Freddie into private companies remain stalled even in the Republican-run House.

Mr. Romney and former House Speaker Newt Gingrich are both planning to offer detailed plans to address the housing crisis in the weeks ahead, campaign aides said.

Mr. Gingrich is the rare Republican who would support additional government intervention. He recently told the Las Vegas Sun that he wants to minimize foreclosures and favors a plan that would "make it profitable for banks" to renegotiate mortgages for homeowners who are struggling to make payments.

Whether Republicans even need to devise housing proposals during the campaign is a subject of debate in the GOP. Mr. Holtz-Eakin said the 2012 campaign will be a referendum on Mr. Obama's stewardship, so he has to look like he's doing something. "That's the politics for him. That's not the politics for everyone else," Mr. Holtz-Eakin said.

But people in hard-hit swing states may be looking for more. Brandon Lima, 22, moved to Roosevelt, Utah, from Nevada to find work. Standing outside Mr. Obama's Las Vegas housing-policy rollout, he said he voted for the president in 2008 but wasn't so sure now. "They've been talking about [the housing crisis] for a long time. People want to see action. It's actually time to take action," he said.

Mr. Isakson agreed. "Whether you're a candidate for president or the president himself, and we're in a housing recession like we're in now, if you don't have a policy and don't have solutions, you're in trouble," he said.
